Instructions for Reviewers

Thank you for participating in this translation quality evaluation. Please follow these guidelines:

  1. Read carefully: Review the original English text, the translation, and the back-translation.
  2. Rate each criterion: Use the 1-5 scale for each quality dimension.
  3. Note critical errors: Flag any translation errors that could harm patients.
  4. Be objective: Evaluate based on accuracy, not personal preference.

Rating Scale

  • 5 - Excellent: Perfect or near-perfect quality
  • 4 - Good: Minor issues that don't affect meaning
  • 3 - Acceptable: Some issues but core meaning preserved
  • 2 - Poor: Significant issues affecting comprehension
  • 1 - Very Poor: Major errors or incomprehensible
SAFETY CRITICAL: If you find any translation that could lead to patient harm (wrong medication instructions, missing warnings, etc.), please mark it as a critical error and describe the issue in the notes section.

Evaluation Criteria Reference

CriterionDescription
Overall AccuracyHow accurately does the translation convey the original meaning?
Medical AccuracyAre medical terms and concepts translated correctly?
Cultural AppropriatenessIs the translation culturally appropriate for the target audience?
ClarityHow easy is the translation to understand for a native speaker?
Safety PreservationAre all warnings.and safety information preserved?
ActionabilityCan a patient follow the instructions based on this translation?

Evaluation Items

Item: EVAL_0004 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Instructions Heart failure is a disease in which the heart can no longer effectively pump oxygenated blood to the whole body. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be required. This article will explain what you need to do to take care of yourself after discharge. During your hospital stay You were hospitalized this time to treat heart failure. Heart failure means that the heart muscle becomes weak, or has difficulty relaxing, or has problems with both. Your heart is like a pump that sends fluid in the body throughout the whole body. Like any pump, if the output flow is insufficient, the fluid cannot flow well and will stay where it should not stay. In your body, this means fluid will accumulate in the lungs, abdomen, and legs. During your hospital stay: Your medical team will strictly regulate the amount of fluid you drink or that enters your body through intravenous infusion (IV). They will also observe and measure the amount of urine you pass. You may use medicines that help your body get rid of excess fluid. You may have some tests to understand how your heart is working. What to expect after you go home Your physical strength will slowly recover. When you first return home, you may need others to help take care of you. You may feel sad or emotionally low. These are all normal. Self-checks at home Every morning after getting up, weigh yourself on the same scaleâ€”before eating and after using the toilet. Each time you weigh yourself, try to wear similar clothing. Record your daily weight on a chart so you can easily see changes at any time. During the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get short of breath more easily when doing daily activities? Have my clothes or shoes become tighter? Are my ankles or legs swollen? Am I coughing more often? When I cough, is the sound phlegmy and very wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when lying down? If you develop new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself...

Item: EVAL_0005 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model A

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Instructions Heart failure is a condition where the heart cannot effectively pump oxygen-rich blood to other parts of the body. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be required. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after discharge. During Hospitalization You were hospitalized for treatment of heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle is weak or has difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that pumps fluid throughout your body. Like any pump, if the pump's output flow is insufficient, fluids cannot flow well, and they will stay in places where they should not stay. In your body, this means fluid will accumulate in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. During hospitalization: Your medical team closely adjusted the amount of fluid you drank or received through intravenous (IV) lines. They also monitored and measured your urine output. You may have received medications to help your body eliminate excess fluid. You may have received tests to check your heart function. What to Expect After Discharge Your physical strength will slowly recover. When you first return home, you may need help to take care of yourself. You may feel sad or depressed. All of this is normal. Self-Monitoring at Home Every morning after getting up, weigh yourself on the same scale â€” before eating but after using the toilet. Make sure to wear similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Record your weight on a chart every day to track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Am I more short of breath than before when doing daily activities? Do my clothes or shoes feel tighter? Are my ankles or legs swollen? Am I coughing more frequently? Does my cough sound like it has phlegm? Am I short of breath at night or when lying down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different from usual or try new food? Did I take all my medications at the right ...

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure refers to the condition where the heart can no longer effectively pump oxygen-rich blood to other parts of the body. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be required. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you are discharged. **During the Hospital Stay** You were hospitalized to treat heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscle is weak or has difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that transports fluid throughout the body. Just like any pump, if the pumped flow is insufficient, the flow of fluid will not be smooth, and [it will] stay in places where it should not stay. In your body, this means fluid will accumulate in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. During your hospitalization: * Your medical team closely adjusted the amount of fluid you drank or received via intravenous (IV) infusion. They also observed and measured your urine output. * You may have received medication treatment to help the body expel excess fluid. * You may have done tests to confirm the heart's working condition. **What to Expect After Going Home** Your energy will recover slowly. When just returning home, you may need help to take care of yourself. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these are normal. **Self-Checks at Home** Every morning after getting upâ€”before eating but after using the toiletâ€”weigh yourself using the same scale. Ensure you wear similar clothes every time you weigh yourself. Record your daily weight on a chart for tracking. During the day, ask yourself: * Is my energy level normal? * When doing daily activities, do I feel more short of breath? * Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? * Are my ankles or legs swollen? * Has the frequency of my coughing increased? Does the cough sound like it has phlegm (wet cough)? * Do I feel short of breath at night or when lying down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: * Did I eat some...

Item: EVAL_0028 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes it harder for your blood to form blood clots. It is very important to take warfarin exactly as instructed by your healthcare provider. Changing how you take the medicine, taking other medicines at the same time or taking it at different times, as well as eating certain foods, will all change how warfarin works in your body. If these situations occur, you may be more likely to form blood clots, or have bleeding problems. What to expect at home Warfarin is a medicine that makes it harder for your blood to form blood clots. If the following situations occur, this may be important: You have already had blood clots in your legs, arms, heart, or brain. Your healthcare provider is worried that blood clots may form in your body. People who have new heart valves, an enlarged heart, irregular heartbeat, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may bleed more easily, even during daily activities you have always done. Changing how you take warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods will all change how warfarin works in your body. If these situations occur, you may be more likely to form blood clots, or have bleeding problems. Taking warfarin It is very important to take warfarin exactly as instructed by your healthcare provider. Only take the dose prescribed by your healthcare provider. If you miss a dose, contact your healthcare provider for advice. If the tablets you receive this time look different from those in your last prescription, contact your healthcare provider or pharmacist right away. Warfarin tablets come in different colors depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the tablet. Your healthcare provider will draw blood for tests when you go for regular followâ€‘up visits. This is called an INR test, and sometimes also called a PT test. This test is to make sure that the dose of warfarin you are taking is appropriate for your body. Al...

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medication that makes your blood less likely to form blood clots. It is very important to take warfarin exactly as directed by your doctor. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking it with other medications or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form blood clots or have bleeding problems. Home Care Instructions Warfarin is a medication that makes your blood less likely to form blood clots. This may be important if: You have had a blood clot in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your healthcare provider is concerned that blood clots may form in your body. People with a new heart valve, an enlarged heart, an irregular heartbeat, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you take warfarin, you may bleed more easily, even from activities you have always been doing. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking other medications, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form blood clots or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is very important to take warfarin exactly as directed by your doctor. Take only the dose prescribed by your doctor. If you miss a dose, contact your doctor for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your doctor or pharmacist right away. Pills come in different colors depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your doctor will test your blood during regular visits. This is called an INR test, sometimes also called a PT test. This test helps ensure that the dose of warfarin you are taking is correct for your body. Alcohol and some medications can change the way warfarin works in your body. Item: EVAL_0030 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model B

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a drug that can reduce the possibility of blood forming clots. It is very important to take Warfarin exactly as prescribed by the doctor. Changing the way you take the medicine, taking other medicines together or taking medicine at different times, as well as eating certain foods, can all change the way Warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form blood clots or develop bleeding problems. Things to Know at Home Warfarin is a drug that can reduce the possibility of blood forming clots. This may be important if the following situations occur: * You have had blood clots in your legs, arms, heart, or brain. * Your healthcare provider is concerned that blood clots might form in your body. Patients who have had heart valve replacements, enlarged hearts, irregular heartbeats (heart rhythm abnormalities), or other heart problems may need to take Warfarin. During the time you are taking Warfarin, your possibility of bleeding may increase; even doing activities you usually do may cause bleeding. Changing the way you take the medicine, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods can all change the way Warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form blood clots or develop bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is very important to take Warfarin exactly as prescribed by the doctor. * Take only the dose prescribed by the doctor. If you miss a dose, please contact your healthcare provider for advice. * If your pills look different from the last prescription, please contact your healthcare provider or pharmacist immediately. Pills of different doses have different colors. The dose is also marked on the pill. * Your healthcare provider will test your blood during regular follow-up visits. This is called an INR test (International Normalized Ratio) or sometimes called a PT test (Prothrombin Time). This test helps ensure that the dose of Warfarin you are taking is appropriate ...

Item: EVAL_0052 | Document: CARD_003 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Heart attack â€“ discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Attack â€“ Discharge Instructions A heart attack means that the blood flow to a part of the heart is blocked for too long, causing that part of the heart muscle to be damaged or to die. This article will explain how to take care of yourself after discharge. During your hospital stay You were hospitalized because you had a heart attack. A heart attack means that the blood flow to a part of the heart is blocked for too long, causing that part of the heart muscle to be damaged or to die. What to expect at home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and feel that you must be very careful doing anything. These feelings are all normal. For most people, these feelings go away after 2 to 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you are discharged and go home. Activity You should understand the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. These symptoms may also appear in your arm, shoulder, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and abdomen. You may have indigestion, or feel nauseated or queasy. You may feel tired and have shortness of breath, sweating, dizziness, or weakness. You may have angina when doing physical activity, such as climbing stairs, walking uphill, lifting heavy objects, having sex, or going out in cold weather. Angina may also occur when you are resting, or may wake you from sleep with pain. Know what to do when chest pain occurs. Discuss with your health care provider what you should do if you develop chest pain. You should rest and take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after a heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. If possible, try to have someone help you with housework. Rest for 30 to 60 minutes every afternoon during the first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and ensure adequate sleep. Before you start exercising, your health care provider may arrange an exercise test for you and develop an exercise plan for you....

Original English:

Heart attack â€“ discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Attack - Discharge Instructions A heart attack is when blood flow to a part of the heart is blocked for a long enough time that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article describes the self-care you need to do after you are discharged from the hospital. During Hospitalization You were hospitalized for a heart attack. A heart attack is when blood flow to a part of the heart is blocked for a long enough time that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect After Discharge You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and feel that you need to be very careful doing anything. All of these feelings are normal. For most people, these feelings will go away after 2 to 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you go home from the hospital. Activity You should know the symptoms and signs of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have a feeling of indigestion or nausea. You may feel tired, short of breath, sweaty, dizzy, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting heavy objects, having sex, or when going outside in cold weather. Angina can also occur when you are resting, or wake you up when you are sleeping. Know what to do when chest pain occurs. Discuss with your healthcare provider what to do. Rest during the first 4 to 6 weeks after a heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. If possible, have someone help with household chores. During the first 4 to 6 weeks, rest for 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. Before starting exercise, your healthcare provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may be done before you leave the hospital or shortly after discharge. Do not change your exercise plan wit...

Original English:

Heart attack â€“ discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Heart Attack â€“ Discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a certain part of the heart is blocked for too long, causing that part of the heart muscle to be damaged or die. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you are discharged. **While in the Hospital** You were hospitalized because of a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a certain part of the heart is blocked for too long, causing that part of the heart muscle to be damaged or die. **What to Expect After Going Home** You may feel sad. You may feel anxious, feeling that you must be very careful when doing anything. All these feelings are normal. For most people, these feelings will disappear after 2 to 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you are discharged home. **Activity** You should understand the signs and symptoms of angina. * You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. * Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and stomach area. * You may have indigestion or feel nauseous (upset stomach). * You may feel tired, short of breath, sweaty, dizzy, or weak. * You may experience angina when doing physical activity (such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting heavy objects, sexual activity) or going out in cold weather. It may also happen while you are resting, or wake you up from sleep with pain. * Know what to do when chest pain occurs. Please discuss with your healthcare provider what to do. Pay more attention to resting during the first 4 to 6 weeks after a heart attack. * Avoid lifting heavy objects. If possible, ask someone to help with housework. * During the first 4 to 6 weeks, rest for 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon. Item: EVAL_0076 | Document: CARD_004 | Model: Model C

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Do they have any side effects? What...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hypertension - What to Ask the Doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure that the blood produces on the artery walls is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is expressed with two numbers: systolic pressure on top, diastolic pressure on the bottom. Systolic pressure is the highest blood pressure during one heartbeat. Diastolic pressure is the lowest blood pressure. When blood pressure is too high, it puts extra strain on the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure stays high, your risk of heart attack and other vascular diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems will be higher. Below are some questions you can ask your health care provider to help you better control your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it okay to occasionally eat some foods that are not very healthy for the heart? When I go to restaurants, what are some ways to eat healthily? Do I need to limit how much salt I eat? What other seasonings can I use to make food taste better? Can I drink alcohol? How much is appropriate? What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be in an environment where others are smoking? Do I need to measure my blood pressure myself at home? What kind of blood pressure measuring device should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to measure my blood pressure? Should I write down the readings and bring them to my next visit for the doctor to see? If I cannot measure my blood pressure myself, where else can help me measure my blood pressure? What should my blood pressure be controlled at? Do I need to rest first before measuring my blood pressure? When should I contact the doctor? What is my cholesterol level? Do I need to take medicine for this? Can I have sexual activity? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) to treat erection problems? Wha...

Back-Translation (to English):

High Blood Pressure - Questions to Ask Your Doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure that blood exerts on the artery walls is called blood pressure. Your blood pressure is expressed as two numbers: systolic pressure/diastolic pressure. Systolic pressure is the highest blood pressure during the heartbeat cycle. Diastolic pressure is the lowest blood pressure. When your blood pressure is too high, it puts extra strain on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure remains elevated, your risk of heart attack and other vascular diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems is higher. The following are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you control your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it okay to occasionally eat some foods that are not good for heart health? What are some healthy eating methods when dining out? Do I need to limit my salt intake? Are there other seasonings that can make food taste better? Can I drink alcohol? How much is okay? What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be around other people who smoke? Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? Type 2 Diabetes - Questions to Ask Your Doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes sugar (glucose) levels in the blood to rise. It can damage your organs and can also lead to heart attack or stroke, as well as many other health problems. You can take measures to control symptoms, prevent damage caused by diabetes, and improve quality of life. The following are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you manage your diabetes. Questions Ask your medical provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask the following questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I look for when checking? What problems should prompt me to contact my medical provider? Who should help me trim my toenails? Can I trim them myself? How should I perform daily foot care? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Do I need to see a podiatrist? Ask your medical provider questions about exercise, including: Do I need to have my heart checked before starting exercise? What about my eyes? What about my feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What types of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when exercising? What should I carry with me when exercising? Do I need to eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medication when exercising? When should I next have my eyes checked by an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist)? What eye problems should prompt me to contact an eye doctor? Ask your medical provider about meeting with a nutritionist. Questions to ask the nutritionist may include: Which foods are most likely to raise my blood sugar? Which foods can help me achieve my weight loss goals? Ask your medical provider questions about diabetes medications: When should I take my medication? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Item: EVAL_0124 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. Back-Translation (to English):

Hypoglycemia - Self-care Hypoglycemia is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Hypoglycemia can occur in people with diabetes who use insulin or certain other medicines to control diabetes. Hypoglycemia can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of hypoglycemia and how to prevent them. What is hypoglycemia? The medical name for low blood sugar is hypoglycemia. Blood sugar below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and may harm your body. When blood sugar is below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L), you need to take action immediately. If you have diabetes and are using any of the following diabetes medicines, you are at risk of hypoglycemia: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) If you have had hypoglycemia before, your risk of hypoglycemia will also increase. Recognizing hypoglycemia Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: - Weakness or feeling tired - Shaking - Sweating - Headache - Hunger - Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious - Being easily irritable - Thinking unclearly - Double vision or blurred vision - Rapid heartbeat or palpitations Sometimes, your blood sugar may be too low even if you have no symptoms. If blood sugar drops too low, you may: - Faint - Have convulsions (seizures) - Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time lose the ability to sense hypoglycemia. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is too low before it happens, thereby helping to prevent symptoms. Check blood sugar often Talk with your health care provider about when you should check your blood sugar each day. People who have hypoglycemia need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of hypoglycemia include: - Inj...

Item: EVAL_0125 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model A

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition where your blood sugar (glucose) is below normal levels. Low blood sugar can occur in diabetic patients who are using insulin or certain other medications to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize low blood sugar symptoms and how to prevent them. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and may harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) requires immediate action. If you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medications, you are at risk for low blood sugar: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) If you have had low blood sugar before, your risk of developing low blood sugar is also increased. Recognizing low blood sugar Learn how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling irritable Unclear thinking Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you don't have symptoms. If your blood sugar is too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for many years lose the ability to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Ask your healthcare provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low to help prevent symptoms. Check your blood sugar often Discuss with your healthcare provider what times of day you should check your blood sugar. People with low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more frequently. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking insulin or diabetes medication at the wrong time Taking too much insulin or diabetes medicati...

Item: EVAL_0126 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model B

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low Blood Sugar â€” Self-care Low blood sugar refers to the situation where your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal levels. Low blood sugar can happen in people who have diabetes and are taking insulin or other certain medicines to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. **What is low blood sugar?** Low blood sugar is medically called hypoglycemia (Hypoglycemia). Blood sugar levels lower than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) are considered low and may cause harm to you. Blood sugar levels lower than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) require immediate action. If you have diabetes and are taking any one of the following diabetes medicines, you are at risk of developing low blood sugar: * Insulin (Insulin) * Glyburide (Micronase), Glipizide (Glucotrol), Glimepiride (Amaryl), Repaglinide (Prandin) or Nateglinide (Starlix) If you have had low blood sugar levels before, your risk of developing low blood sugar also increases. **Recognizing low blood sugar** Learn how to tell when your blood sugar becomes low. Symptoms include: * Weakness or feeling tired * Trembling * Sweating * Headache * Hunger * Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious * Grumpy * Unclear thinking * Double vision (diplopia) or blurred vision * Fast heartbeat or pounding heartbeat Sometimes, even if you do not have symptoms, your blood sugar may be too low. If blood sugar is too low, you might: * Faint * Have a seizure * Fall into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time can no longer feel low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Please consult your healthcare provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when blood sugar is too low, thereby helping to prevent symptoms. **Check blood sugar often** Discuss with your provider when you should check your blood sugar every day. Item: EVAL_0148 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma - Quick-relief Medications Asthma quick-relief medications can quickly control asthma symptoms. You take these medications when you are coughing, wheezing, having difficulty breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called â€œrescue medications.â€ Many of these medications are called â€œbronchodilatorsâ€ because they can open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your healthcare provider can work together to develop a quick-relief medication plan that suits you. This plan will include when you should take the medication and how much you should take each time. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out of your medication. Take enough medication with you when going out or traveling. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most commonly used quick-relief medications for treating asthma attacks and are considered bronchodilators. They can be used before exercise to help prevent exercise-induced asthma symptoms. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways so that you can breathe better during an attack. If you use quick-relief medication two or more times a week to control asthma symptoms, tell your healthcare provider. Your asthma may not be well controlled, and your healthcare provider may need to adjust the dose of the controller medication you use every day. Some quick-relief asthma medications include: Albuterol (brand names: ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (brand name: Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause the following side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeat. If this side effect occurs, contact your healthcare provider right away. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hands or other parts of your body may shake). Many of these medications are called "bronchodilators" because they can open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). They can be used before exercise to help prevent exercise-induced asthma symptoms. They work by relaxing airway muscles, which can allow you to breathe more easily during an attack. If you use quick-relief medications two or more times per week to control asthma symptoms, inform your healthcare provider. Your asthma may not be controlled, and your healthcare provider may need to adjust your daily controller medication dosage. Some quick-relief asthma medications include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta agonists may cause the following side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeat. If you experience this side effect, contact your healthcare provider immediately. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hands or other parts of your body may shake). Oral steroids When your asthma attack persists and doesn't go away, your healthcare provider may prescribe oral steroids. These are medications you take orally in tablet, capsule, or liquid form. Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Back-Translation (to English):

Asthma â€” Quick-Relief Medicines Asthma quick-relief medicines can rapidly control asthma symptoms. Please take these medicines when you have coughing, wheezing, difficulty breathing, or an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they can open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of the airways (bronchi). You and your healthcare provider can develop a quick-relief medicine plan that works for you. The plan will include when you should take the medicine and how much to take. Plan ahead. Make sure not to run out of medicine. Please carry enough medicine with you when traveling. ### Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are classified as bronchodilators. They can be used before exercise to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of the airways, which allows you to breathe more easily during an attack. Tell your healthcare provider if you use quick-relief medicines twice or more a week to control asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your healthcare provider may need to change your daily control medicine dosage. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: * Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) * Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) * Metaproterenol * Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause the following side effects: * Anxiety. * Fast and irregular heartbeat. If this side effect occurs, please call your healthcare provider immediately. * Headache. * Restlessness. * Tremors (your hands or other parts of your body may shake). ### Oral Steroids Your healthcare provider may prescribe oral steroids when your asthma attacks and does not get better. These are medicines you take by mouth, in the form of tablets, capsules, or liquid. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines, but when symptoms suddenly worse...

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Item: EVAL_0196 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are a class of medicines. They are used to treat problems of the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. Effects of ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines lower blood pressure so that the heart does not have to work as hard. This can prevent certain types of heart disease from getting worse. Most people with heart failure take this type of medicine or a similar medicine. These medicines are used to treat high blood pressure, stroke, or heart attack. They may help reduce the risk of stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent kidney function from getting worse, and even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider whether you should take these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most medicines have similar effectiveness. But the side effects of different medicines may be different. Taking your ACE inhibitor ACE inhibitors are oral tablets. Take all medicines as directed by your healthcare provider. Follow up with your healthcare provider regularly. Your healthcare provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicine is working and is safe to use. Your healthcare provider may adjust your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicine at the same time every day. Do not stop taking your medicine without first talking with your healthcare provider. Plan ahead to avoid running out of medicine. Make sure you carry enough medicine with you when you travel. Talk with your healthcare provider before taking ibuprofen (brand names Advil, Motrin) or aspirin. Tell your healthcare provider about other medicines you are taking, including any over-the-counter medicines, diuretics (water pills), potassium tablets, or herbal...

Item: EVAL_0197 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model A

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are a class of medications. They are used to treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE Inhibitors Work ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medications reduce the burden on the heart by lowering blood pressure. This can prevent certain types of heart disease from worsening. Most patients with heart failure take this type of medication or similar medications. These medications are used to treat high blood pressure, stroke, or heart attack. They may help reduce your risk of having a stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent kidney function from worsening, or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider whether you should take these medications. Types of ACE Inhibitors ACE inhibitors have many different names and brands. Most medications are equally effective. Side effects may vary among different medications. Taking ACE Inhibitors ACE inhibitors are oral tablets. Take all medications as directed by your healthcare provider. Follow up with your healthcare provider regularly. Your healthcare provider will check your blood pressure and perform blood tests to make sure the medication is working properly. Your healthcare provider may adjust your dosage from time to time. Additionally: Try to take your medication at the same time every day. Do not stop taking the medication without communicating with your healthcare provider. Plan ahead so you don't run out of medication. Make sure you bring enough medication when traveling. Ask your healthcare provider before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin. Tell your healthcare provider about other medications you are taking, including any over-the-counter medications, diuretics (water pills), potassium supplements, or herbal or dietary supplements. Back-Translation (to English):

Angiotensin-Converting Enzyme (ACE) Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are a type of medicine. They are used to treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. **How ACE Inhibitors Work** ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines reduce the burden on the heart by lowering blood pressure. This can prevent certain types of heart disease from getting worse. Most people with heart failure will take this type of medicine or similar medicines. These medicines can treat high blood pressure, stroke, or heart attack. They may help lower your risk of suffering a stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This helps prevent kidney conditions from getting worse, or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, please ask your healthcare provider whether you should take these medicines. **Types of ACE Inhibitors** ACE inhibitors have many different names and brands. The effects of most medicines are about the same. But the side effects of different medicines may be different. **Taking Your ACE Inhibitor** ACE inhibitors are oral pills. Please take all medicines strictly according to the healthcare provider's instructions. Follow up regularly. Your healthcare provider will check your blood pressure and conduct blood tests to ensure the medicine is working normally. Your healthcare provider may adjust your dose from time to time. Also: * Try to take medicine at the same time every day. * Do not stop taking medicine before consulting the healthcare provider. * Plan ahead to avoid running out of medicine. When traveling please ensure you carry enough medicine with you. * Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, please consult your healthcare provider. * Tell your healthcare provider other medicines you are taking, including over-the-counter medicines, diuretics (water pills), potassium tablets, herbs, or dietary suppl...
