Item: EVAL_0220 | Document: EMER_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Discharge You were hospitalized because of a stroke. A stroke is when the blood flow to a part of the brain is interrupted. After you go home, follow all of your healthcare providerâ€™s instructions about self-care. The information below can serve as a reminder. While you were in the hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain and to help your heart, lungs, and other important organs recover. After your condition became stable, your healthcare provider performed tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and to prevent another stroke. You may have stayed in a unit that specializes in helping patients recover after a stroke. What to expect after you go home Because a stroke can cause damage to the brain, you may find that you have problems with: Changes in behavior Doing simple tasks Memory Movement on one side of the body Muscle spasms Attention Sensation or awareness of a part of the body Swallowing Speaking or understanding others Thinking Vision in one side of the visual field (hemianopsia) You may need other people to help with daily activities that you could do by yourself before. You may need physical therapy or occupational therapy to regain the ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is very common, because you need to learn to adapt to these changes. Depression may appear soon after the stroke, or it may appear up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive a vehicle unless your healthcare provider has given you permission. Activity and movement After a stroke, being active and doing everyday things may become difficult. Make sure your home environment is safe. Ask your healthcare provider, therapist, or nurse whether changes are needed in your home to make daily activities easier. Learn how to prevent falls and how to make using the bathroom safer. Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Discharge Instructions You were hospitalized for a stroke. A stroke is when blood flow to part of the brain is interrupted. Please follow the self-care instructions from your healthcare provider after discharge. The following information is for your reference. During Your Hospital Stay First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain and help the heart, lungs, and other vital organs recover. After your condition stabilized, healthcare providers performed examinations and began treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent it from happening again. You may have received treatment in a specialized unit that helps stroke patients recover. What to Expect After Discharge Because a stroke can cause damage to the brain, you may notice the following problems: Behavioral changes Difficulty completing simple tasks Memory problems Difficulty moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Attention problems Sensation or awareness problems in parts of the body Difficulty swallowing Difficulty speaking or understanding others Thinking problems Loss of vision on one side (hemianopia) You may need help from others to complete daily activities you could previously do independently. You may need physical therapy or occupational therapy to regain these functions. Depression after stroke is relatively common and is a normal response as you adjust to life changes. Depression may occur shortly after a stroke or may occur within 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive a car without permission from your healthcare provider. Activity After a stroke, moving around and completing daily tasks may become difficult. Make sure your home environment is safe. Ask your healthcare provider, therapist, or nurse about how to modify your home environment to make daily activities more convenient. Learn how to prevent falls and how to ensure bathroom safety. Family members and caregivers may need to assist with the following: Help with exercises to keep elbow...

Item: EVAL_0222 | Document: EMER_001 | Model: Model B

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Discharge You were hospitalized for treatment due to a stroke. A stroke occurs when blood flow to a certain part of the brain stops. After returning home, please follow the medical staff's instructions for self-care. The following information can serve as a reminder. **During the hospital stay** First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other vital organs recover. After your condition stabilized, medical staff performed examinations for you and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent another stroke. You may have stayed in a special ward dedicated to helping stroke patients recover. **What to expect after returning home** Since a stroke may cause damage to the brain, you may notice problems in the following areas: * Behavior changes * Doing simple tasks * Memory * Moving one side of the body * Muscle spasms * Attention * Sensation or perception in a certain part of the body * Swallowing * Speaking or understanding others * Thinking * Vision on one side (hemianopia) You may need assistance from others to complete daily activities that you could perform alone before the stroke. You may need to receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore the ability to perform these functions. As you learn to adapt to these changes, the appearance of depression after a stroke is quite common. It may appear shortly after the stroke, or it may appear up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive without permission from medical staff. **Activity and Mobility** After a stroke, moving around and doing daily chores may be difficult. Ensure your home environment is safe. Consult your medical staff, therapist, or nurse on how to modify the home environment to perform daily activities more easily. Learn what measures you can take to prevent falls, and ensure bathroom use is safe. Family members and caregivers may need to provide help in the following ...

Item: EVAL_0244 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical Wound Care â€“ Open Wounds An incision is a cut made in the skin during surgery, also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are very small, and some are relatively long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you have had. Sometimes, the incision may split open. This may happen along the entire incision, or only in a part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to use stitches (needle and thread) to sew it closed again. What to expect at home If your surgeon has not stitched the wound closed again, you will need to care for the wound at home, because it may take some time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom up. Dressings can help absorb drainage and prevent the surface skin from closing before the wound underneath has filled in. Proper handwashing It is very important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or, you can wash your hands with water and soap by following these steps: 1. Remove all jewelry from your hands. 2. Wet your hands under running warm water, with your fingers pointing down. 3. Add soap and rub your hands together for 15 to 30 seconds (you can silently sing â€œHappy Birthdayâ€ or the â€œAlphabet Songâ€ once). Clean under your fingernails at the same time. 4. Rinse thoroughly. 5. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often you need to change the dressing. When preparing to change the dressing: - Wash your hands before touching the dressing. - Make sure all supplies are within reach. - Prepare a clean work surface. To remove the old dressing: - Carefully and slowly peel the tape away from the skin. - Put on clean (non-sterile) medical gloves, grasp the old dressing, and remove it. - If the dressing sticks to the wound, moisten the dressing first and then try again, unless your doctor has specifically asked you to pull it off dry. - Place the old dressing into a plastic bag and set it ...

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical Wound Care - Open Wound An incision is a cut made in the skin during a surgical procedure. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, while others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you are having. Sometimes, an incision will open up. This may happen along the entire incision, or only part of it may open. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with stitches (sutures). What to Know About Home Care If your surgeon does not close your wound again with stitches, you will need to care for the wound at home because healing may take time. The wound will heal from the bottom up. Dressings help absorb drainage and prevent the skin from closing too early before the wound underneath has filled in. Proper Hand Washing It is very important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands as follows: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Point your hands downward and wet them under warm running water. Add soap and wash for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" once). Also clean under your fingernails. Rinse thoroughly. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your healthcare provider will tell you how often to change the dressing. When ready to change the dressing: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure all supplies are ready and within reach. Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from the skin. Put on clean (non-sterile) medical gloves, grasp the old dressing and remove it. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your healthcare provider instructs you to remove the dressing dry. Place the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after removing the old dressing. Wound Care You can use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around the wound: Use saline (salt water) or mild soapy water. Di...

Item: EVAL_0246 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model B

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Surgical Wound Care â€” Open Wound An incision is a cut made on the skin during the surgery process, also known as a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and some are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you receive. Sometimes, the incision will split open. This can happen to the whole incision or only a part. Your surgeon may decide not to use sutures (stitches) to sew it back together again. **Things to Note After Going Home** If your surgeon did not use sutures to sew your wound again, you need to perform care at home, because wound healing may take time. The wound will heal from the bottom up. The dressing helps absorb drainage fluid and prevents the skin from closing before the inside of the wound heals well. **Correct Hand Washing Method** It is very important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based waterless hand sanitizer. Or, you can follow the steps below to wash your hands: * Take off all jewelry on your hands. * Wet your hands with warm running water, fingers pointing down. * Apply soap and scrub hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing the "Happy Birthday" song or the "Alphabet song" once). Also clean under the fingernails. * Rinse well. * Dry with a clean towel. **Removing Old Dressing** Your healthcare provider will tell you how often to change the dressing. When preparing to change the dressing: * Clean your hands before touching the dressing. * Make sure all supplies are close at hand. * Prepare a clean work surface. * Remove the old dressing: * Carefully loosen the tape from the skin. * Use a clean (no need to be sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and remove it. * If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. * Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. * Clean your hands again after removing the old dressing. **Wound Care** You can use a gau...

Item: EVAL_0268 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement Surgery - Discharge Instructions You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This material will tell you how to care for your new hip joint after you are discharged home. While in the hospital You had hip replacement surgery, in which all or part of the hip joint was replaced with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to expect after you go home When you are discharged home, you should already be able to walk with a walker or crutches and not need too much help. Most people no longer need these assistive devices 2 to 4 weeks later. Ask your surgeon when you can stop using crutches. You should also basically be able to dress yourself with only a small amount of help, and be able to get in and out of bed or a chair by yourself. You should also basically be able to use the toilet by yourself. You need to be especially careful to avoid dislocation of the artificial hip joint, especially in the first few months after surgery. You need to learn some exercises to strengthen the new hip joint and take some special precautions. For 1 to 2 weeks after you are discharged home, someone needs to stay with you at home. You will need help from others to prepare meals, bathe, move around the house, and complete other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to gradually return to your previous level of activity. But you need to avoid certain sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like rugby or soccer. However, you should be able to do some low-impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and playing golf. Home setup The height of your bed should be appropriate: when you sit on the edge of the bed, your feet can touch the floor; at the same time, the bed should be high enough so that when you sit on the edge, your hip joint is higher than your knees. You do not necessarily need a hospital bed, but the mattr...

Item: EVAL_0269 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: Model A

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement Surgery - Discharge Instructions You had surgery to replace part or all of your hip joint with an artificial joint (called a prosthesis). This article tells you how to care for your new hip after discharge. During Hospitalization You had hip replacement surgery to replace part or all of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Know After Discharge When you are discharged, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people no longer need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Ask your surgeon when you can stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with a small amount of help, and be able to get in and out of bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without needing much help. You need to be careful not to dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You need to learn exercises to make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. After discharge or leaving the rehabilitation center, you will need someone to stay with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks. You will need someone to help prepare meals, bathe, move around the room, and do other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous activity level. You will need to avoid some sports, such as alpine skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low-impact activities like hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and playing golf. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough that when you sit on the edge of the bed, your feet can touch the ground. Your bed should also be high enough that when you sit on the edge of the bed, your hips are higher than your knees. You may not need a medical bed, but the mattress should be firm. Remove tripping hazards from your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cables from areas you pass through when walking from one room...

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement - Discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a "prosthesis". This article will tell you what you need to do to care for your new hip joint after discharge. **During the Hospital Stay** You had hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. **What to Expect at Home** When going home, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without much help. Most people no longer need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Please consult your surgeon about when you can stop using crutches. You should be able to dress yourself with only a little help, and get in and out of bed or chairs yourself. You should also not need much help when using the toilet. You need to be careful not to dislocate your artificial hip joint, especially in the first few months after surgery. You need to learn exercises that strengthen the new hip joint and take special precautions. For 1 to 2 weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center, you need someone to stay with you at home. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous level of activity. You need to avoid certain sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low-impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and playing golf. **Home Setup** The height of your bed should allow your feet to touch the ground when you sit on the edge of the bed, while ensuring your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge of the bed. You may not need a hospital bed, but the mattress should be firm. Remove tripping hazards in your home. * Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose electrical wires or cords in areas you pass through when walking from one room to another. Remove loose...

Item: EVAL_0290 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a disease where the heart cannot effectively pump oxygen-rich blood to other parts of the body. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be needed. This article discusses how you need to take care of yourself after discharge. During Hospitalization You were hospitalized to treat heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscle becomes weak or has difficulty relaxing, or both occur simultaneously. Your heart is a pump that transports liquid throughout the body. Just like any pump, if the pumped flow is insufficient, liquid cannot flow normally and will stagnate in places it shouldn't. In your body, this means liquid will accumulate in the lungs, abdomen, and legs. During hospitalization: * Your medical team closely adjusted the amount of liquid you took in through oral intake or intravenous infusion (IV). They also observed and measured your urine output. * You may have received medications that help the body expel excess liquid. * You may have received tests that examine the condition of heart function. What Happens After Discharge Your energy will slowly recover. When you first return home, you may need others to help take care of you. You may feel sad or depressed. These are all normal. Home Self-Monitoring Every morning after getting up, before eating, but after using the toilet, weigh yourself with the same scale. Ensure you wear similar clothing each time you weigh. Record your weight on a chart daily to track. During the day, ask yourself: * Is my energy level normal? * Do I get short of breath more easily when performing daily activities? * Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? * Are my ankles or legs swollen? * Am I coughing more frequently? Does the cough sound wet? * Am I short of breath at night or when lying down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: * Did I eat different foods than usual or try new foods? * Did I take all medications on time and in the correct amount?...

Item: EVAL_0298 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

--- --- Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medication that makes blood less likely to form clots. Be sure to take Warfarin exactly as prescribed by your doctor. Changing the way you take it, taking it at the same time or different times as other medications, and eating certain foods can all change the way Warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form clots or have bleeding problems. Home Precautions Warfarin is a medication that makes blood less likely to form clots. Taking this medication may be important in the following situations: - You have had blood clots in your legs, arms, heart, or brain. - Your healthcare provider is concerned that blood clots may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, an enlarged heart, irregular heartbeat, or other heart problems may need to take Warfarin. While taking Warfarin, you may bleed more easily, even activities you have always engaged in may cause bleeding. Changing the way you take it, taking other medications, and eating certain foods can all change the way Warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form clots or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin Be sure to take Warfarin exactly as prescribed by your doctor. - Only take the dose prescribed for you by your doctor. If you miss a dose, contact your doctor for advice. - If the tablet looks different from the medication you filled last time, contact your doctor or pharmacist immediately. Tablet colors vary by dose, and the dose is also marked on the tablet. - Your doctor will do blood tests for you at regular follow-up visits. This test is called an INR test, sometimes also called a PT test. This test helps ensure that the Warfarin dose you are taking is suitable for your body's needs. Item: EVAL_0306 | Document: CARD_003 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Heart attack â€“ discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Attack - Discharge A heart attack refers to blood flow to a certain part of the heart being blocked for a sufficiently long time, causing part of the heart muscle to be damaged or die. This article discusses how you need to take care of yourself after discharge. During Hospitalization You were hospitalized due to a heart attack. A heart attack refers to blood flow to a certain part of the heart being blocked for a sufficiently long time, causing part of the heart muscle to be damaged or die. Expected Situation After Discharge Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious, as if you must be very careful in doing things. All these feelings are normal. For most people, these feelings will disappear after 2 to 3 weeks. When discharged home, you may also feel tired. Activity You should understand the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in the chest. You may also notice these symptoms in the arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people will also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and stomach. You may experience indigestion or stomach discomfort. You may feel tired, short of breath, sweating, dizzy, or weak. You may experience angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting heavy objects, sexual activity, or when going out in cold weather. It may also occur when you are resting, or wake you up during sleep. Understand what to do when chest pain occurs. Discuss with your healthcare provider what should be done. Relax and rest for the first 4 to 6 weeks after heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. If possible, ask others to help with housework. Rest for 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon during the first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and ensure sufficient sleep. Before starting exercise, your doctor may have you undergo an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before discharge or shortly after discharge. Do not change your exerci...

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Hypertension - Questions to Ask Your Doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of the blood on the arterial walls is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is expressed with two numbers: systolic pressure/diastolic pressure. Systolic pressure is the highest value of blood pressure in the cardiac cycle. Diastolic pressure is the lowest value of blood pressure. When blood pressure is too high, it brings extra pressure to the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure remains persistently high, you will face higher risks of heart attack and other vascular diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you control your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower blood pressure? Do I need to monitor blood pressure at home? What kind of device should I purchase? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I record it and bring it to my next appointment? If I cannot measure blood pressure myself, what other places can I measure it? What should my blood pressure reading be? Do I need to rest before measuring blood pressure? When should I contact my healthcare provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medication? Can I have sexual activity? Item: EVAL_0330 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hypoglycemia - Self-care Hypoglycemia refers to a condition where blood sugar (glucose) is below normal levels. Hypoglycemia can occur in patients using insulin or certain other medications to control diabetes. Hypoglycemia can cause dangerous symptoms. Please learn how to recognize the symptoms of hypoglycemia and how to prevent it. What is hypoglycemia? Hypoglycemia is called hypoglycemia. Blood sugar levels below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) are considered hypoglycemia and may cause harm to you. Blood sugar levels below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) require immediate action. If you have diabetes and are using any of the following diabetes medications, you have a risk of developing hypoglycemia: - Insulin - Glyburide (Micronase), Glipizide (Glucotrol), Glimepiride (Amaryl), Repaglinide (Prandin) or Nateglinide (Starlix) If you have had hypoglycemia before, your risk of developing hypoglycemia also increases. Recognizing Hypoglycemia You need to know how to tell if your blood sugar is dropping. Symptoms include: - Weakness or feeling tired - Trembling - Sweating - Headache - Hunger - Feeling restless, nervous, or anxious - Feeling irritable - Unclear thinking - Double vision or blurred vision - Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes even if you have no symptoms, your blood sugar may be too low. If blood sugar is too low, you may: - Faint - Seizures - Fall into a coma Some people with long-term diabetes can no longer sense hypoglycemia. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Please consult your healthcare provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is too low, thereby helping to prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Frequently Discuss with your provider what times of day you should check your blood sugar. People with hypoglycemia need to check their blood sugar more frequently. The most common causes of hypoglycemia include: - Using insulin or diabetes medication at the wrong time - Using too much insul...

Item: EVAL_0337 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Back-Translation (to English):

Asthma - Quick-relief medications Asthma quick-relief medications act quickly and can control asthma symptoms. When you cough, wheeze, have difficulty breathing, or have an asthma attack, you can take these medications. They are also called rescue medications. Many of these medications are called "bronchodilators" because they can open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your healthcare provider can formulate an effective quick-relief medication use plan for you. This plan will include when you should take it and how much to take. Plan ahead. Ensure medications do not run out. Carry sufficient medication with you when traveling. Short-acting beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most commonly used quick-relief medications for treating asthma attacks and are considered bronchodilators. They can be used before exercise to help prevent exercise-induced asthma symptoms. They work by relaxing airway muscles, which allows you to breathe more smoothly during an attack. If you use quick-relief medications twice or more per week to control asthma symptoms, please inform your healthcare provider. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to adjust the dosage of your daily control medications. Some quick-relief asthma medications include: â€¢ Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) â€¢ Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) â€¢ Metaproterenol â€¢ Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause the following side effects: â€¢ Anxiety. â€¢ Rapid and irregular heartbeat. If you experience this side effect, please call your healthcare provider immediately. â€¢ Headache. â€¢ Restlessness. â€¢ Tremor (your hands or other parts of your body may shake). Oral steroids When your asthma attack persists without relief, your healthcare provider may prescribe oral steroids. These are oral medications, including tablets, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medications, but are typically used for 7 to 14 d...

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Discharge You were hospitalized due to a stroke. A stroke refers to the cessation of blood flow to a part of the brain. After being discharged home, please follow the self-care instructions from medical staff. The following information can serve as a reminder. During Hospitalization First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other vital organs recover. After your condition stabilized, medical staff conducted examinations and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent another stroke. You may have stayed in a special ward specifically designed to help stroke patients recover. Situations That May Occur After Discharge Due to damage that the stroke may cause to the brain, you may notice the following problems: - Behavioral changes - Difficulty completing simple tasks - Memory - Difficulty moving one side of the body - Muscle spasms - Difficulty concentrating - Sensation or awareness of a part of the body - Difficulty swallowing - Difficulty speaking or understanding others - Thinking ability - Loss of vision in one side of the visual field (hemianopia) You may need help to complete daily activities that you could do alone before the stroke. You may need to receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after stroke is quite common because you need to learn to adapt to these changes. It may appear soon after the stroke, or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive a car without permission from medical staff. Activity After a stroke, activity and completing daily tasks may be difficult. Ensure your home is safe. Please consult your doctor, therapist, or nurse about how to modify your home to make it easier to complete daily activities. Understand what measures you can take to prevent falls, and ensure bathroom safety. Family members and caregivers may need to assist with: - Performing exercises to ke...

Item: EVAL_0370 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Open Surgical Wound Care An incision is a cut made on the skin during surgery, also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, some are longer. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you receive. Sometimes incisions will split open. This may happen to the entire incision or only part of the incision. Your surgeon may decide not to reclose it with sutures (stitches). Home Care Instructions If your surgeon did not reclose the wound with sutures, you need to perform care at home, because wound healing may take some time. The wound will heal from bottom to top. Dressings help absorb exudate, and prevent the skin surface from closing prematurely before the wound bottom heals. Proper Hand Washing It is important to clean hands before changing dressings. You can use alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or, you can follow these steps to wash hands: 1. Remove all jewelry from hands. 2. Place hands downward under warm water flow to rinse. 3. Apply soap, wash hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday Song" or "Alphabet Song" once). Also clean under nails. 4. Rinse thoroughly. 5. Dry with a clean towel. Removing Old Dressing Medical staff will tell you how often to change dressings. To prepare for changing dressings: 1. Clean hands before touching dressing. 2. Ensure all supplies are at hand. 3. Prepare a clean work surface. Remove old dressing: 1. Carefully peel tape off from skin. 2. Use a clean (non-sterile) medical glove to grasp the old dressing and remove it. 3. If dressing sticks to wound, please wet it before trying again, unless your medical staff instructs you to peel it off directly. 4. Place old dressing in plastic bag and set aside. 5. Clean hands again after removing old dressing. Caring for Wound You can use gauze pads or soft cloth to clean skin around wound: â€¢ Use normal saline (saline solution) or mild soapy water. â€¢ Soak gauze or cloth in normal saline or soapy water, use it to gently wipe skin. â€¢ Try to clear away all exud...

Item: EVAL_0378 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement Surgery - Discharge You underwent surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint (called a prosthesis). This article tells you how to care for your new hip joint after discharge. During Hospitalization You underwent hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. Expectations After Discharge By the time you are discharged, you should be able to walk with the aid of a walker or crutches without needing too much help. Most people no longer need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Please consult your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help, and be able to get in and out of bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without needing too much help. You need to be careful not to dislocate the artificial hip joint, especially within the first few months after surgery. You need to learn exercises to strengthen the new hip joint and take special precautions. After discharge or leaving the rehabilitation center, you need someone to stay with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks. You need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and performing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous activity level. You need to avoid certain sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like rugby and soccer. But you should be able to do low-impact activities such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golf. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough that your feet can touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge of the bed. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Remove tripping hazards from your home. Learn to prevent falls. â€¢ Remove loose wires or cords from the areas wher...
